tool
1	"""PyTorch reference for a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit (batch=1).
2	
3	Correctness oracle, NOT the fast baseline (baseline.py) or the SOTA ceiling.
4	One repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B -- 3 KDA (gated-delta linear attn)
5	layers + 1 MLA (multi-head latent attention) layer, each + a 64-expert MoE FFN
6	-- decoded one token at a time, with the big projection weights stored as
7	**W4A16** (int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation; the AWQ/GPTQ
8	format that open-source inference actually ships).
9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode
… +360 more lines

tool
1	"""Optimized-PyTorch W4A16 baseline for the Kimi-Linear decode unit.
2	
3	The floor the solution must beat -- NOT the oracle (reference.py). Same int4
4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.
12	"""

tool
1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2	
3	Per-step correctness, not a chained trajectory: for each seed and a couple of
4	context lengths, the reference and the solution are given an IDENTICAL fed
5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""

Let me understand the problem: 1. We need to build a megakernel (single GPU kernel launch per decode step) for a Kimi-Linear hybrid block: - 4 layers: [KDA, KDA, KDA, MLA] - Each followed by 64-expert MoE FFN (top-8 + 1 shared) - Hidden 2304 - W4A16 int4 quantized weights with group-size 128 asymmetric dequant 2. Single kernel launch per `step()` call - either Triton `@triton.jit` single grid launch or CUDA `load_inline` `__global__`. 3. Baseline materializes bf16 weights; we need fused dequant-GEMV. Let me think about the entire computational graph for one token step: **Per KDA layer:** - x_normed = rmsnorm(x, attn_norm) [2304] - q = x_normed @ Wq [2304 -> 4096] (int4) - k = x_normed @ Wk [2304 -> 4096] (int4) - v = x_normed @ Wv [2304 -> 4096] (int4) - conv on q, k, v: window of 4 (prev 3 + current), causal depthwise conv with weights conv_w[0/1/2] (3, 4096, 4), silu activation. State cq/ck/cv (3, 4096) updated to win[1:]. - g = -softplus(x_normed @ Wg) [2304 -> 4096] (int4), per-channel log-decay - beta = sigmoid(beta_proj(x_normed)) [2304 -> 32] (bf16 linear) - S (32, 128, 128): S = S * exp(g)[:, :, None]; pred = sum_k S[h,k,:]*k[h,k]; S += beta[h] * k[h,k] * (v - pred)[h,:]; o = sum_k S[h,k,:]*q[h,k] - o_proj: o (4096) @ Wo [4096 -> 2304] (int4) - h = x + o_proj_out **Per MLA layer:** - x_normed = rmsnorm(x, attn_norm) - q = x_normed @ Wq [2304 -> 32*192 = 6144] (int4) - kv = x_normed @ Wkv_a [2304 -> 512+64=576] (int4) - c_kv = kv[:512], k_rope = kv[512:] - rope at position pos on q_rope (each head's last 64) and k_rope - append c_kv, k_rope to caches (pos+1 total) - kvb = c_kv_cache @ Wkv_b [(pos+1), 512] @ [512 -> 32*256=8192] (int4) — this is the big growing cost! Or absorb: scores = q_nope @ (W_k part of kv_b) ... Actually the standard MLA absorb: k_nope[l,h,d] = sum_c c_kv[l,c] * Wkv_b[c, h*256+d]. scores[l,h] = sum_d q_nope[h,d]*k_nope[l,h,d] + sum_r q_rope[h,r]*k_rope[l,r]. Two options: (a) materialize k_nope per token per head: cost (pos+1) * 512 * 8192 MACs — huge: at 16k context: 16384*512*8192 = 68.7 GFLOP. Wait that's 512*8192 per token = 4.2M MACs per token, times 16384 tokens = 68.7 GMACs = 137 GFLOP. And weight stream is just 512*8192*0.5 bytes = 2MB int4 + scales/zeros. Hmm, actually the compute cost dominates: 137 GFLOP at ~50 TFLOPs bf16 would be ~3ms. That's too slow. (b) absorb: scores[l,h] = sum_d q_nope[h,d] * sum_c c_kv[l,c] Wkv_b[c, hp] = sum_c (sum_d q_nope[h,d] Wkv_b[c, hp]) * c_kv[l,c]. Let q_absorbed[h,c] = sum_d q_nope[h,d] Wkv_b_up[c, h,d] where Wkv_b_up is the k_nope part [512, 32*128]. Then scores[l,h] = sum_c q_absorbed[h,c] * c_kv[l,c] + rope part. Cost of absorb: q_nope (32*128) needs to be up-projected: for each head, q_absorbed[h,c] (512) = q_nope[h,:] (128) @ W_up[c... wait Wkv_b[c, h*256 + d], so W_up[h] is [512, 128], q_absorbed[h] = W_up[h].T @ ... = sum over d of q_nope[h,d] * W_up[h][c,d]. That's 32 heads * 512 * 128 = 2.1M MACs — cheap. Then scores = q_absorbed (32, 512) @ c_kv_cache.T (512, L) = 32*512*L MACs. At L=16384: 268M MACs. Fine. Then p (L, 32) softmax. Output o[h,dv] = sum_l p[l,h] * v[l,h,dv] where v[l,h,dv] = sum_c c_kv[l,c] * Wkv_b[c, h*256 + 128 + dv] (the v part of kv_b). Absorb again: o[h,dv] = sum_c (sum_l p[l,h] c_kv[l,c]) W_v[h][c,dv] = sum_c ctx2[h,c] * W_v[h][c,dv]. ctx2 = p.T @ c_kv_cache: 32*512*L MACs. Then o = ctx2 (32,512) @ W_v[h] (512,128) per head: 2.1M MACs. So absorb reduces the growing part from 137 GFLOP to ~1 GFLOP. This is THE right call for long context. The catch: Wkv_b is int4 and group-quantized along the 512 "in" dim. Absorbing through q means dequant into an intermediate... but we can still dequant on the fly. Hmm wait — but q_absorbed = q_nope @ dequant(W_up). q_nope depends on x (normed input), so we can't precompute. But the dequant of W_up is 512*8192 int4 = 2MB, streamed once. The absorb restructures which weight-multiplies-which-activation, but the weight is streamed once either way. So the fused kernel streams Wkv_b (2MB int4) and computes both q_absorbed and o through it. Also note the correct output must match reference in cosine similarity >= 0.98. The reference materializes kvb per token (well, per cache entry) in fp32. Precision-wise, absorb changes the order of operations; with bf16/fp32 accumulators, cosine similarity should stay very high (values match numerically to ~1e-3 relative). Since the weights are random with std 0.02 and everything is synthetic, numerical differences will be tiny. Cosine 0.98 is generous. Hmm, wait — one important subtlety: reference computes scores in fp32 from k_nope = c_kv @ Wkv_b in fp32. Solution computing q_absorbed = q_nope @ W_up in fp32: mathematically identical result, different FP rounding. Should easily exceed 0.98 cosine. **MoE per layer:** - x_normed = rmsnorm(h, moe_norm) - router: bf16 linear 2304 -> 64, softmax, top-8, renormalize * 2.446 - shared expert + 8 routed: - gate: x @ W_gate [2304 -> 1024] (int4) - up: x @ W_up [2304 -> 1024] (int4) - down: silu(gate)*up @ W_down [1024 -> 2304] (int4) - h = h + sum w_e * down_e + shared Weight bytes per layer: - KDA q/k/v/g: 4 * (2304*4096*0.5 B) = 4*4.72MB = 18.9MB... wait 2304*4096 = 9.44M params *0.5B = 4.72MB each. So q,k,v,g = 18.87MB. o_proj: 4096*2304 = 4.72MB. Total KDA attn: ~23.6MB. - MLA: q_proj 2304*6144 = 14.2M params * 0.5 = 7.08MB; kv_a 2304*576 = 663KB... 2304*576=1.33M params=0.66MB; kv_b 512*8192 = 4.19M params = 2.1MB; o_proj 4096*2304 = 4.72MB. Total MLA: ~14.5MB. - MoE: per expert gate/up: 2304*1024 = 2.36M params each; down: 1024*2304 = 2.36M. Per expert 3 * 2.36M = 7.08M params = 3.54MB int4. 9 experts (8 routed + 1 shared) = 31.9MB per layer. (All 64 experts stored = 226MB but only 9 streamed.) Per-layer streamed: KDA ~23.6 + MoE 31.9 + scales/zeros. Scales/zeros: ng * N * 2 bytes * 2 = e.g. q_proj: 18 groups * 4096 * 2B * 2 = 294KB vs weight 4.72MB — 6%. OK. 3 KDA layers: 3 * 55.5MB = 166MB. MLA layer: 46.4MB. Total ~213MB per token. Plus MLA cache read: L * 576 * 2B = at 16384: 18.9MB (c_kv + k_rope), at 8192: 9.4MB, 2048: 2.4MB. Plus KDA state S read+write: 32*128*128*4B(fp32) = 2MB read + 2MB write per KDA layer = 12MB total. Actually S in fp32: 32*128*128*4 = 2.1MB. Reference keeps S in fp32? init_state: S = randn(...) * 0.05 — float32 by default on cuda. Then st["S"] = S where S computed in float. Yes S is fp32. Total traffic at ctx 16384: ~213 + 19 + 12 = 244MB. At 1.8 TB/s: 136 µs → ~7.4k tok/s. The baseline materializes bf16 weights: reading int4 + writing bf16 + reading bf16 = 0.5 + 2 + 2 = 4.5 bytes per param vs 0.5 → 9x more traffic for weights ~(0.505*9)/0.505... roughly 9x on the weight portion: ~213MB*9 = 1.9GB + rest → ~1ms per token → ~1000 tok/s. So a perfect solution gets ~7-9x speedup. We need to be efficient. Actually let me compute the baseline more carefully: - Weights: for each linear, read int4 (0.5B/param), write bf16 (2B), read bf16 (2B): 4.5B/param. Params per layer: KDA 47.2M, MoE per streamed expert 7.08M*9=63.7M → 110.9M per KDA layer; MLA: 29M + 63.7M = 92.7M. Total: 3*110.9+92.7 = 425.4M params * 4.5B = 1.91GB. Plus cache reads 19MB. At 1.8TB/s → ~1.07ms → ~900-1000 tok/s. Our target: ~244MB → 136µs → 7350 tok/s → speedup ~7.9x. Realistically we might hit 60-80% of peak bandwidth in a single fused kernel → maybe 4-6x. Also, scales+zeros traffic: scales are bf16 (ng, out) = for q_proj KDA: 18*4096*2 = 147KB, same for zeros → 6.25% overhead of the 4.72MB. Fine, included above roughly. Now the big challenge: SINGLE KERNEL LAUNCH. The entire 4-layer forward in one kernel. This means: - Grid sync between stages (cooperative groups / grid.sync() in CUDA, or in Triton... Triton doesn't have a portable grid sync). Options: 1. CUDA C++ via load_inline with cooperative groups grid.sync(). Requires launching with cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel. This gives us full control: one __global__ kernel, grid.sync() between stages. All SMs participate. This is the standard way to build such a megakernel. 2. Triton: no grid.sync. Would need atomic-counter-based software barriers (spin on atomics). Possible but hairy. Actually people do implement global barriers in Triton via atomics + spinning with `tl.atomic_add` and while loops. Triton supports while loops. But it's risky (deadlock if grid sync assumptions break; need all blocks resident). With cooperative launch guarantee absent, a software barrier requires all blocks co-resident — so grid size must be ≤ what fits simultaneously. On RTX PRO 6000 (188 SMs? Let me check — GB202: RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell has 188 SMs and 96GB GDDR7), we can launch e.g. 188 blocks * 1024 threads with everything resident if occupancy allows (1 block/SM with 1024 threads). A software global barrier via atomics works if we guarantee residency. Triton kernels can't use cooperative launch, so we must self-limit the grid. Actually, CUDA with cooperative groups is much cleaner and safer. Let me plan a CUDA load_inline solution. Wait, but introspection: "one @triton.jit grid launch, or one CUDA __global__ built with load_inline". So CUDA via load_inline is explicitly allowed, with cooperative launch presumably OK since it's still a single kernel launch (cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel launches ONE kernel; it's not a graph). Let me think about whether grid sync via cooperative groups is acceptable: yes — it's literally one kernel launch. That's the canonical way to write a megakernel in CUDA. Let me now check GPU properties. Plan of the kernel: **Stage 0**: input hidden x (bf16, 2304). Residual stream kept in global or shared. Overall structure per block (layer) L = 0..3: 1. RMSNorm1: xn = rmsnorm(x, attn_norm) 2. Attention (KDA or MLA) with int4 GEMVs 3. Residual: h = x + attn_out 4. RMSNorm2: hn = rmsnorm(h, moe_norm) 5. MoE with router + 9 experts int4 GEMVs 6. Residual: x = h + moe_out The whole thing is bandwidth-bound on streaming weights. The X vector is tiny (2304). Each linear: out[n] = sum_k x[k] * W[k,n]. Key design: distribute work across the GPU. We have 188 SMs (need to check). We'll use a lot of blocks. The GEMV work per layer: KDA attention GEMVs: q,k,v,g: each 2304→4096; o: 4096→2304; beta: 2304→32 (small, bf16). Between q/k/v/g GEMV and the S update, need conv and gating — all elementwise-ish, then S update is per-head 128x128: each head reads S (64K fp32 = 256KB), updates it, writes back. 32 heads → 2MB read + 2MB write per layer. The S update for head h: read S[h] (128x128), k[h] (128), v[h] (128), g[h], q[h], beta[h]. Compute pred = S^T k (128 ops per column...). Per head: 128x128 grid of (k-row, v-col): pred[v] = sum_k S[k,v]*k[k]; S[k,v] = S[k,v]*exp(g[k]) + beta*k[k]*(v[v]-pred[v]); o[v] = sum_k S_new[k,v]*q[k]. We need to be careful about the ordering semantics: reference: ``` S = st["S"] * g.exp()[:, :, None] pred = (S * k[:, :, None]).sum(1) S = S + beta[:,None,None] * k[:,:,None] * (v - pred)[:,None,:] o = (S * q[:,:,None]).sum(1) ``` So decay applied first, then pred from decayed S, then rank-1 update, then o from updated S. o uses the UPDATED S. Fine: for each head: - for each column v: pred[v] = sum_k S[k,v]*gexp[k]*k[k] - S_new[k,v] = S[k,v]*gexp[k] + beta*k[k]*(v[v]-pred[v]) - o[v] = sum_k S_new[k,v]*q[k] We can do per (k-row) streaming: assign block per (head, v-chunk): read S chunk, compute. MLA: L up to 16384. Attention over cache with absorbed form: - Phase A: q,kv GEMVs (2304→6144, 2304→576), rope, cache append. - Phase B: q_absorbed[h,c] = sum_d q_nope[h,d] * W_up_dequant[c, h*128+d]; also we'd need W_up (512, 4096) int4 streamed: 1MB. And W_v for later. - Phase C: scores[l,h] = (sum_c q_abs[h,c]*c_kv[l,c] + sum_r q_rope[h,r]*k_rope[l,r]) * scale; softmax over l per h. - Phase D: ctx[h,c] = sum_l p[l,h]*c_kv[l,c]; o[h,dv] = sum_c ctx[h,c]*W_v_dequant[c, h*128+dv]; then o_proj (4096→2304). Alternative for scores: use flash-attention style online softmax to avoid 2 passes over c_kv. We read c_kv twice (score pass + weighted sum pass): L*512*2B*2 = 33.5MB at 16k. Online softmax fuses into one pass. But with absorbed form, note ctx[h,c] = sum_l p[l,h] c_kv[l,c] — that's weighted sum over the latent dim — exactly like flash attention's V aggregation where "V" = c_kv (512-dim). And scores from q_abs (512-dim "K"). So it IS flash attention with d_k = d_v = 512, H=32, plus a separate rope 64-dim contribution to scores. Online softmax handles it in one pass over the cache. Numerical: online softmax matches reference softmax to fp precision. Fine. But wait, there's a subtlety with the absorbed form + online softmax: reference computes p in fp32 softmax over full scores. Online algorithm gives same result. Good. MLA cache append: we need to write the new c_kv and k_rope at position pos. With in-place state update: state "c_kv" is a tensor we cat; in our solution we can pre-allocate a max-size cache and write in place? The state dict carries c_kv of growing length; check compares st_sol[mla]["c_kv"] cosine AFTER one step: shape (ctx+1, 512). And then benchmark calls step repeatedly `steps` times (decodes multiple tokens) so our cache must keep growing across step calls. Approach for cache management: in our `Model`, intercept state dicts: replace with our own persistent buffers? The interface: step(hidden, state) where state is a list of dicts created by reference.init_state. We must return updated state such that subsequent steps work with OUR step (benchmark loops model.step(h, st)), and check compares st_sol[mla]["c_kv"] (the returned state) with reference's — must have shape (ctx+1, 512) and matching values. Also reference model consumes its own state separately. Simplest: our step returns state where keys map to genuine (ctx+1, ...) tensors. If we keep an internal persistent big buffer, we can return a VIEW state["c_kv"][:pos+1]. check does cosine on the view — fine. benchmark loops: each step receives state (with views possibly); we can just ignore the passed state's storage and use our own, but safest to actually read/write a persistent buffer and return views. But careful: check calls sol_model.step(h.clone(), st_sol) once per (seed, ctx) with st_sol = reference.init_state(...). Between (seed,ctx) pairs, our persistent buffer must be RESET to the fed state. So our step must start from the state passed in, not from leftover internal buffers. So: at the start of step, we must load the MLA cache from the passed-in tensors (they're fresh each call with length ctx... in benchmark, after our own previous step, the state passed is what WE returned — a view into our buffer or fresh cat'd tensors). Simplest robust approach: treat state tensors as source of truth each call. At step start, copy the MLA cache (up to ctx tokens) into a pre-allocated workspace of max size (ctx+steps headroom)... but the copy itself costs bandwidth ~19MB at 16k — significant (19MB/244MB ≈ 8%). Alternative: write directly into the returned tensor: since reference.init_state creates exact-length tensors and we then need to append 1 row, we could allocate new (ctx+1) tensors each step, cat: that costs a copy too but it's on small data relative... 19MB copy vs 244MB total ≈ same 8%. Hmm, but wait — benchmark timing loop calls model.step 16-32 times in a row, each step re-copying cache → the copy happens every step. If instead we detect "this state's storage is already our persistent buffer at the right offset" we can skip the copy. Trick: on first step for a given state object, allocate persistent big buffer (ctx + 1024 cap), copy in; return state with c_kv = buf[:ctx+1] (a view). On next step, recognize state["c_kv"].data_ptr() == buf.data_ptr() (it's our view) and skip the copy, just extend length counter. If a foreign state arrives (data_ptr doesn't match), rebase: copy into buf. This gives zero-copy steady state in benchmark and correct rebase behavior in check (each check iteration passes fresh tensors → data_ptr differs → rebase). Resizing: if pos+1 exceeds cap, allocate bigger buffer and copy. Good. Same trick for KDA state S: S is fixed size (32,128,128) fp32 2MB per layer, 3 layers = 6.3MB. Actually also let me reconsider TRITON briefly — the barrier story: Triton CAN do grid-wide spin barriers (all blocks resident, tl.atomic_add + spin loop with tl.load volatile). Let me first carefully write down the exact design for the Triton megakernel. **Overall kernel structure (Triton):** ```python @triton.jit def megakernel( # static weight pointer table (device buffer of int64 addresses? or pass ~40 pointers) ..., # mutable per-step: x_in pointer, x_out pointer (=persistent), pos, state pointers ... ): # program_id = block id ``` Triton launch: kernel[grid](args, num_warps=8). Grid barrier via atomics: ```python def grid_barrier(bar_ptr, phase_ptr, ...): ``` Barrier design: simplest correct software barrier with sense reversal: - persistent device int32: `bar_count`, `bar_gen` - on barrier: tid==0 of each block: `tl.debug_barrier()` (block __syncthreads) first; then old = atomic_add(bar_count, 1, sem="acq_rel"); if old == num_blocks-1: bar_count=0 (via atomic_xchg relaxed), atomic_add(bar_gen, 1, sem="release"); else spin: while tl.load(bar_gen, volatile) == gen: pass; - every block tracks gen locally (increments its own counter after each barrier). Actually simpler: gen seen = private variable; target = gen+1. Spin loop in Triton: `while` loops are supported. Volatile load of a scalar: tl.load(ptr, volatile=True). OK. Memory ordering: in Triton, atomic with sem="acq_rel" gives fence. Before signaling, need all block writes visible: the atomic acq_rel on the same address by all participants plus volatile reads of gen... Standard pattern works on GPUs (people do it). To be safe I'll use `tl.atomic_add(..., sem='acq_rel')` for the arrival and `sem='acquire'` spin loads... Actually Triton atomics accept sem argument: "acquire", "release", "acq_rel", "relaxed". Loads: tl.load(..., volatile=True) has no sem; there's also `tl.load` with `cache` modifiers. I might use atomic_add(ptr, 0, sem='acquire') as an acquire-load for the flag. Alright, stage work inside: each block has `pid = tl.program_id(0)`; loop over stages with `if/elif` on a Python-level... no — stage sequencing must be uniform: write stages sequentially with barriers between, each stage implements work-stealing loops `for item in range(pid, n_items, NUM_BLOCKS)`. Triton constraints: all code is per-program (block). tl operations work on block-level tiles. A "warp item" isn't directly expressible — Triton abstracts at block granularity... you CAN just use block-level tile ops: for GEMV: cols = offs_n (block tile of e.g. 128 cols), loop over k in chunks, load w_q tile (k_chunk//2, 128) uint8, unpack via bit ops, x tile from smem?? Triton doesn't expose smem directly — tl.load x chunk into registers as tile (K_CHUNK,) broadcast... x in tiles: load x (K_CHUNK,) from global each iteration — these loads hit L2/L1 cached (x is 9KB, hot) fine. GEMV in Triton style: ``` acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), tl.float32) for k0 in range(0, K, K_CHUNK): xk = tl.load(x_ptr + k0 + tl.arange(0, K_CHUNK)) # fp32 (K_CHUNK,) w = tl.load(wq_ptr + (k0//2 + tl.arange(0, K_CHUNK)//2)[:,None]*N + offs_n[None,:]) # uint8 (K_CHUNK, N)... ``` Wait w_q row for k: byte row = k//2 — loads for k and k+1 share the byte. Unpack: wbyte (K_CHUNK//2, N): lo = wbyte & 0xF, hi = wbyte >> 4. Then acc += sum over the K_CHUNK dim: (x_lo[k] * lo[k]) + (x_hi[k] * hi[k]) — as (CH, N) * (CH, 1) → tl.sum. tl.sum over CH with (CH, N) tile → fine, tensor-core-free SIMT math... Triton will try to use FMA vector ops; the reduction tl.sum axis=0 over a (256, 128) tile = tree洗牌 — generates decent code. +"""Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit - SINGLE-KERNEL megakernel solution.
+
+The entire per-token forward (KDA layers, MLA layer, MoE FFNs, norms, residuals,
+state updates) is fused into ONE cooperative CUDA kernel launched once per
+step(). Weights are streamed as packed int4 and dequantized inline in the GEMV
+loops (no bf16 weight matrix is ever materialized). MLA uses the absorbed form
+(q projected up through kv_b, cache read once with an online softmax), which is
+what makes long context cheap.
+
+The inter-stage dependencies inside a token are handled by a grid-wide software
+barrier (single atomic counter + sense target), which is why the kernel is
+launched with cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel (all blocks co-resident). The only
+GPU work in step() besides this one launch is, on the FIRST step after a fresh
… +1385 more lines

Better: overlap the gate and up gemv k-loops in ONE pass (no syncthreads between): compute both partials in one k-sweep: acc_g + acc_u simultaneously, halving the loop/sync overhead: the weight reads for gate stream, then up stream — same total — but 1 loop instead of 2 (one sync instead of 2)... marginal. The REAL issue is per-block rate 6GB/s and only 72 blocks. To utilize 188 blocks: split k: items (slot, cg, khalf): 144 items: each computes partial over half K with fp32 partial → needs ATOMIC merge or extra reduce stage... atomicAdd to acts directly: acts written via silu(g)*u — needs BOTH → two passes different. ALTERNATIVE: interleave MORE work into stage D: define two gemv sweeps in one k-loop (gate+up together): ``` for g in groups: load scales_gate/zeros_gate..., scales_up... (12 loads); 8×(pk_g, pk_u) loads; accumulate acc_g, acc_u ``` → 2 arrays of acc in registers (8 fp32). Same total bytes streamed, one fused loop, one reduce+sync for both. Then acts = silu(acc_g)*acc_u locally: BEST structure (removes one sync-wait + intermediate gate smem). Also halves the #items needed per slot... items: 9 slots × 8 cg = 72 — still 72 blocks max... but they're 2× fatter now (each does 2 GEMVs' traffic)... hmm wait — no, merging doesn't change traffic per item: before: 2 gemv calls serially; after: 1 loop streaming both — time halves-ish due to better pipelining (the two sweep latencies were serialized before). ALSO unroll-wise, 16 loads in flight per warp per group → better MLP!! THAT should push D close to the 1.2TB/s envelope IF blocks were enough. Blocks: 72 busy of 188 — per-block needs 300KB: still 49µs... UNLESS blocks do better than 6.1GB/s with deeper MLP: 16 loads/group × 128B... 2KB/warp in flight → 20GB/s/block?? → 15µs. Justify: in-flight bytes per block = 8 warps × 16 rows/burst × 128B = 16KB → 16KB/0.8µs = 20GB/s ✓ so fused gate/up could give ~2-3× on D (65 → ~25µs). APPLY THE SAME IDEA TO STAGE A (qkvg): q,k,v,g are 4 GEMVs sharing the same xsm — fuse into groups of 2 (or 4!): sweep q&k/v&g... wait they're separate weight TENSORS: fuse pairs: (q,k) and (v,g)? or all 4 in one k-sweep: 4 tensors × same group: loads 4×8 rows... 32 loads in flight — register pressure: 4 acc arrays = 16 regs + x 16 = ... ~fine. items: (tensor-pair, cg)... with 4-fuse: items per 128-col cg: 32 items × 4-tensor... cover all items in 32 blocks?! just 32 blocks — BAD. +---
+name: kernelbench-gpu-lock-environment
+description: The KernelBench mega/hard benchmarks run inside a gpu-lock-exec wrapper — process-level GPU serialization; how to work with it
+metadata:
+  type: project
+---
+
+On the shared KernelBench GPU boxes (observed on the RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell box, 188 SMs, CC 12.0, 134MB L2), the problem directory's `bin/python` is a bash wrapper, not a binary: it routes through `bin/gpu-lock-exec` (an `flock` on `KBH_GPU_LOCK`) so benchmark agents serialize GPU access. Consequences:
+
+- Plain `python` jobs can wait minutes at startup behind other tenants' lock holds (looks like a hang).
+- Real benchmark/timing measurements happen during the agent's lock-exclusive windows — those are the only trustworthy timing numbers.
+- For pure file edits/text manipulation, use `/usr/bin/python3` (has torch in dist-packages, skips the wrapper entirely).
+- ncu has no perf-counter permission on these boxes; nsys ok; use `%globaltimer` in-kernel instrumentation instead, and Triton/CUDA microbenchmarks for bandwidth probes.

+---
+name: kimi-linear-decode-solution-architecture
+description: Working megakernel solution structure for the 02_kimi_linear_decode problem (slurm of design decisions and traps hit)
+metadata:
+  type: project
+---
+
+Problem: batch-1 W4A16 decode of a Kimi-Linear motif [KDA,KDA,KDA,MLA]+64-expert MoE on RTX PRO 6000, staged from `02_kimi_linear_decode`. Scored as baseline-latency/solution-latency; one cooperative-kernel launch per `step()` (judge-gated).
+
+Working design (solution.py):
+- One `cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel` per step (188 blocks × 256 thr), 25→22 software grid barriers via a single monotonic atomic counter with host-precomputed targets (host tracks `bar_base`, adds `nbc*nb` per call).
+- MLA is ABSORBED (q projected up through kv_b → scores/ctx computed directly on the 576-dim latent cache; cost stays constant in context length).
+- Streaming math: int4 GEMVs never materialize bf16; `gemv128` (128-col tile, 8-warp k-split, group-major reload of scales/zeros at group boundaries, `#pragma unroll 1` on the outer group loop) hits ~1.2TB/s in isolation; `gemv128_dual` fuses gate+up / q&k / v&g for 2x in-flight bytes.

What if I RESTRUCTURE to bigger COL tiles per item (CG=256 → warp covers 256 cols via uint2)? The right fix for starved stages is K-SPLIT items (partial GEMVs + gmem partial/atomic accumulation): items = (cg, kchunk): e.g. stage C: 18 cg × 4 kchunk (1024 k each) = 72 items: partials atomicAdd fp32 into out... need rounding AFTER: h = x + out: roundings by the LAST kchunk-arriver (per-col atomic count check) — doable via per-col-group arrival counters + last-arriver does the residual add & rounding. But atomic ordering vs bf16 rounding semantics... reference rounding happens once — we can accumulate fp32 fully then last-arriver rounds ✓ EXACT.
